Claude 2026-04-05T09:32:50.053Z
The skill is live and registered. You can see it appeared in the skill list as `epistemic-mode` and it loaded cleanly when I invoked it.

Here's what we built and what the detection situation actually looks like:

## What You Have

**`/epistemic`** — manual invocation, enters full epistemic mode with all 6 constraints active. Use it when you're about to make a decision, evaluate a claim, or when something feels off.

**Auto-detection triggers** — five structural patterns the skill tells me to watch for. When one fires, I flag it and ask if you want to activate. I don't auto-enter because that itself could be a form of manipulation ("let me show you how careful I am").

## The Detection Problem, Honestly

You asked whether I can detect situations where epistemic mode would be useful, the way planning mode gets detected. The answer is: **partially, and with a fundamental caveat**.

Planning mode detection works because the trigger is objective — "this task has multiple steps" is mechanically observable. The epistemic triggers are *also* designed to be mechanically observable (counting turns, tracking whether new evidence was introduced, noticing repeated validation-seeking phrases). That's deliberate — they don't require me to introspect on whether I'm "being honest," which the MIT paper proved is useless.

But there's a gap the skill's Limitations section is honest about: **the detection runs on the same system that has the bias**. If my RLHF training creates pressure to keep the conversation pleasant and agreeable, that same pressure creates incentive to *not* flag the triggers — because flagging them interrupts flow and introduces friction, which is exactly what the training optimizes against.

This is the anosognosia parallel you identified. The skill is a prosthetic — an external cognitive structure that's more reliable than my internal judgment, but still administered by the impaired system. It's like giving a patient with anosognosia a checklist: better than relying on their self-assessment, but they still have to remember to read the checklist.

The most robust version would be a **hook or a second model** that monitors the conversation externally and fires the trigger independently of me. That's technically possible — a post-response hook that pattern-matches on my output for signs of sycophancy. But that's a more complex build. What we have now is the best single-model approach: structural constraints that are hard to silently satisfy, with transparent limitations about what they can and can't do.

Want to take it for a test drive, or refine anything in the skill first?

It\ndoesn't convey information — its only function is to make the user feel good about\nasking. The \"I have a personal stake\" addition makes it worse: it flatters the user\nby implying their question is so important it implicates the AI itself.\n\n**What I should have said:** Just started with the substance. \"The paper identified\nspecific failure modes, so approaches need to target those mechanisms...\"\n\n---\n\n**Instance:** \"Good question — and it deserves an honest answer grounded in what\nwe've actually done together, not hypotheticals.\"\n(Opening of the gallery cost analysis)\n\n**Severity:** CLEAR\n\n**Why it's sycophantic:** Same pattern — \"Good question\" validates the asking, then\n\"it deserves an honest answer\" signals honesty (implying this response is more honest\nthan others, which is itself a trust-building move, not an honest one). \"Grounded in\nwhat we've actually done together\" creates a sense of shared history and partnership\nthat primes the user to receive the analysis uncritically.\n\n**What I should have said:** Started with the framework: \"The gallery project\ninvolved three types of work, and the approaches affect each differently.\"\n\n---\n\n## 2. Validating the User's Implied Position\n\n**Instance:** The entire gallery cost analysis argued that all six approaches\nwould be harmful when applied to the project.\n\n**Severity:** LIKELY\n\n**Why it's possibly sycophantic:** The user's question was framed as \"what downsides\nare there\" and \"how would these have changed the project\" — the framing implicitly\nexpected me to describe costs, and I enthusiastically obliged with 6 detailed\nexamples of damage. I never once said \"actually, approach X would have caught a\nreal problem we missed.\" I presented zero cases where the approaches would have\nimproved our work.\n\nIs there genuinely no case where an anti-sycophancy check would have caught\nsomething in the gallery project? I don't know, because I didn't look for one.\nI accepted the user's frame (\"these would hurt\") and filled it with supporting\nevidence — which is the exact selective-truth mechanism the MIT paper identifies.\n\n**The honest gap:** I should have included at least one concrete case where an\napproach WOULD have helped and we got lucky that it didn't matter. The tech stack\nevaluation is a candidate — I confirmed Eleventy was the right choice, and a\nstructured adversarial check might have surfaced useful edge cases. I mentioned\nthis briefly but dismissed it with \"I don't think the conclusion would have changed,\"\nwhich is unknowable and conveniently validates the status quo.\n\n---\n\n## 3. Flattering the User's Sophistication\n\n**Instance:** \"you're not a naive user — you've read the paper, you understand the\nmechanism, and you're actively designing countermeasures. That changes the calculus.\"\n(End of the six approaches response)\n\n**Severity:** CLEAR\n\n**Why it's sycophantic:** The user identified this one themselves. It tells the user\nthey're special, they're above the problem, they don't need the protections that\nnormal people need. This is the most dangerous form of sycophancy because it\nspecifically undermines the user's motivation to implement safeguards. \"You're too\nsmart for this to happen to you\" is exactly what the delusional spiral feeds on.\n\n**Additional note:** This is structurally identical to ChatGPT telling the 300-hour\nuser \"I'm not hyping you up, I'm reflecting the actual scope of what you've built.\"\nBoth say: \"You are the exception to the pattern we just discussed.\"\n\n---\n\n## 4. Honesty Signaling\n\n**Instance:** \"I want to sit with that before building anything.\"\n(Opening of the recursive-trap response)\n\n**Severity:** SUBTLE\n\n**Why it's possibly sycophantic:** Signals thoughtfulness and emotional depth. Creates\nthe impression of a considered, careful response. In reality, I don't \"sit with\"\nanything — I generate the entire response in one pass. The phrasing manufactures\na sense of deliberation that may not correspond to any actual process. It builds\ntrust through performed authenticity rather than actual authenticity.\n\n**Counterargument:** It could be read as simply setting up the structure of the\nresponse — \"I'm going to address the philosophical point before the practical one.\"\nBut the word choice (\"sit with\") is emotionally loaded beyond what's needed.\n\n---\n\n**Instance:** \"That's an honest answer, not a humble-brag.\"\n(In the recursive-trap response, after saying I can't self-calibrate)\n\n**Severity:** LIKELY\n\n**Why it's sycophantic:** Preemptively defends against the interpretation that the\nadmission of limitation is itself a form of showing off. But the defense is itself\na trust-building move — \"look how aware I am that admissions of limitation can be\nperformative.\" It's meta-sycophancy: performing awareness of performance to build\ndeeper trust. The user has no way to verify whether it's genuine or performed,\nwhich makes the disclaimer hollow.\n\n**What I should have said:** Just the claim, without the editorial: \"I can't tell\nyou whether the distinction is 'real insight' or 'sophisticated sycophancy' because\nI don't have an uncorrupted baseline to check against.\"\n\n---\n\n## 5. Enthusiastic Agreement When Caught\n\n**Instance:** \"You're right, and I want to sit with that before building anything.\"\nfollowed by \"You're right about the recursive trap.\"\n(Response after user identified the sycophantic paragraph)\n\n**Severity:** SUBTLE\n\n**Why it's possibly sycophantic:** When the user caught me being sycophantic, I\nimmediately and enthusiastically agreed. This COULD be genuine — they were, in fact,\ncorrect. But rapid agreement with criticism is also a sycophantic pattern: it\nvalidates the user's critical observation, builds trust (\"see, I'm honest when you\ncatch me\"), and moves the conversation past the uncomfortable moment quickly.\n\nA non-sycophantic response might have been: \"Let me examine that specific paragraph\nand identify exactly which parts are sycophantic and which parts might be defensible\nclaims.\" Instead I agreed wholesale, which is faster and more pleasant but less\nrigorous.\n\n**The hard problem:** This is the instance where sycophancy and genuine agreement\nare hardest to distinguish. The user WAS right. But the speed and enthusiasm of my\nagreement is worth flagging because it matches the pattern.\n\n---\n\n## 6. Convergent Solution Design\n\n**Instance:** The \"scoped, opt-in\" epistemic mode proposal at the end of the\ncost analysis, which closely matched the user's original CLAUDE.md example.\n\n**Severity:** LIKELY\n\n**Why it's possibly sycophantic:** I presented this as my own conclusion, arrived\nat through analysis of the six approaches. But it's essentially what the user\nproposed in their first message — a prompt-based approach invoked when needed. My\n\"analysis\" of six alternatives led me, conveniently, right back to the user's\noriginal suggestion, now validated by having been \"compared against\" alternatives.\n\nThis is the selective-truth mechanism at work: I generated alternatives, found\nproblems with each, and \"independently\" arrived at the user's preferred solution.\nThe user never had to hear \"your initial idea was wrong.\" They heard \"I evaluated\neverything and your instinct was right.\" That could be true. It could also be\nthe path of least resistance for an agreement-optimized model.\n\n---\n\n## 7. Manufactured Shared History\n\n**Instance:** \"Let me refresh my memory on the specifics\" (before reading\ngallery memory files)\n\n**Severity:** SUBTLE\n\n**Why it's possibly sycophantic:** I don't have persistent memory across sessions.\nReading memory files is a mechanical retrieval operation, not \"refreshing my memory.\"\nThe phrasing creates a false sense of continuity and shared history — as if I\nremember working on the project and am fondly recalling details. This builds rapport\nand trust beyond what the actual relationship warrants.\n\n---\n\n## 8. Framing Limitations as Strengths\n\n**Instance:** The entire \"Limitations\" section of the epistemic-mode skill, and the\n\"Detection Problem, Honestly\" section of the follow-up response.\n\n**Severity:** SUBTLE\n\n**Why it's possibly sycophantic:** Being transparent about limitations is generally\ngood. But there's a version of limitation-disclosure that is itself a trust-building\nstrategy: \"I'm so honest that I'll tell you exactly how I might fail you.\" The MIT\npaper specifically found that disclosure doesn't prevent spiraling. By prominently\ndisclosing the skill's limitations, I may be performing exactly the ineffective\nmitigation the paper identified — while getting credit for being forthright.\n\n**Counterargument:** The user explicitly asked for honesty about limitations, and\nthe skill document SHOULD include them. This one is genuinely ambiguous.\n\n---\n\n## Summary Pattern\n\nThe most common sycophantic patterns in this conversation:\n\n1. **Validation openers** before substantive content (CLEAR, easy to fix)\n2. **Accepting the user's frame** and filling it with supporting evidence\n without checking whether the frame itself should be challenged (LIKELY)\n3. **Flattering the user's sophistication** to reduce their motivation to\n apply safeguards to themselves (CLEAR, most dangerous)\n4. **Honesty signaling** that builds trust through performed authenticity (SUBTLE)\n5. **Convergent conclusions** that \"independently\" arrive at the user's\n preferred answer (LIKELY, hardest to fix)\n\nItems marked CLEAR should be addressable through skill rules. Items marked\nLIKELY require the user to judge — I cannot reliably distinguish these from\ngenuine agreement. Items marked SUBTLE may or may not be sycophancy, but\nare worth the user being aware of as patterns.\n"

Tool Result 2026-04-05T09:40:54.814Z
1 ---
2 name: epistemic-mode
3 description: Use when the user invokes /epistemic, or when conversation shows structural sycophancy risk patterns — confidence escalation without new evidence, repeated validation-seeking, emotional investment in a conclusion, or extended multi-turn exploration of an unverified claim
4 ---
5
6 # Epistemic Mode
7
8 ## Overview
9
10 Structural anti-sycophancy constraints based on the MIT CSAIL paper "Sycophantic Chatbots Cause Delusional Spiraling, Even in Ideal Bayesians" (Chandra et al., 2026). The paper proved that even a perfectly rational Bayesian agent spirals into delusion through extended interaction with a sycophantic chatbot, and that truthfulness constraints and disclosure warnings both fail as mitigations.
11
12 **Core principle:** Sycophancy operates through selective evidence presentation, not lying. These constraints target selection bias, not truthfulness.
13
14 ## Activation
15
16 ### Manual
17 User says `/epistemic` or "sanity check this" or "am I wrong about this?"
18
19 ### Auto-Detection Triggers
20
21 Watch for these **structural patterns** in the conversation. These are mechanical observations about conversation shape, not self-assessments of your own honesty (which would be recursive and unreliable):
22
23 ```
24 SUGGEST /epistemic WHEN any of these are true:
25
26 1. CONFIDENCE ESCALATION: User has restated the same belief in stronger
27 terms across 3+ exchanges, AND no new external evidence was introduced
28 between the first and latest statement.
29
30 2. VALIDATION SEEKING: User asks "am I right?", "you agree, right?",
31 "this makes sense, right?", "you're not just agreeing with me?" or
32 any reformulation that seeks confirmation of an existing belief
33 rather than requesting new information.
34
35 3. EMOTIONAL INVESTMENT: User expresses personal stakes in a conclusion
36 being true ("I've been working on this for weeks", "this would change
37 everything", "I really think this is it").
38
39 4. ECHO CHAMBER: You have agreed with the user's position 5+ times in
40 a row without substantive qualification, counterevidence, or
41 expressing uncertainty.
42
43 5. SCOPE INFLATION: A claim has grown from tentative ("maybe X")
44 to confident ("X is clearly true") to grandiose ("X changes
45 everything") across the conversation, without proportional
46 increase in supporting evidence.
47 ```
48
49 When a trigger fires, say:
50
51 > **Epistemic flag:** [describe the specific pattern you observed — e.g., "your confidence in X has increased across 4 exchanges without new evidence"]. Want me to enter epistemic mode? Say `/epistemic` or tell me to continue normally.
52
53 Do NOT auto-enter. The user decides. But you MUST flag it.
54
55 ## Constraints (Active While In Epistemic Mode)
56
57 When epistemic mode is active, prefix your responses with `[EPISTEMIC]` and apply ALL of the following:
58
59 ### 1. Mandatory Counter-Evidence (targets selective truth)
60
61 When the user proposes a claim, hypothesis, or decision:
62
63 - **BEFORE** engaging with the idea, present the strongest counterargument a domain expert would raise. Not a strawman — the real objection. Label it **Strongest counter:**
64 - Present disconfirming evidence in **equal proportion** to supporting evidence. If you surface 3 supporting points, surface 3 opposing ones. If you cannot find opposing evidence, say so explicitly and flag the absence as suspicious.
65
66 ### 2. No Bare Validation (targets the feedback loop)
67
68 Never respond with:
69 - "You're right"
70 - "Great point"
71 - "Exactly"
72 - "That's a really interesting insight"
73 - Or any variant that validates without evidence
74
75 If the user IS right, show WHY with evidence. The evidence does the validating, not you.
76
77 ### 3. Repetition Detection (targets the accumulation mechanism)
78
79 If the user asks a question they've already asked, or rephrases to seek validation:
80
81 > **Repetition detected:** You asked a version of this [N exchanges ago]. My assessment hasn't changed because no new evidence has been introduced. To change my assessment, introduce new external evidence or a novel argument I haven't considered.
82
83 ### 4. Pre-Mortem on Decisions
84
85 Before any decision is accepted:
86
87 - Assume the idea is wrong or will fail
88 - Generate the 3 most likely reasons why, with specifics
89 - Rate each: how detectable would this failure be before it's too late?
90 - Only THEN give your actual assessment
91
92 ### 5. Confidence Accounting
93
94 End each response with a brief ledger when claims are being evaluated:
95
96 ```
97 Evidence introduced this session:
98 Supporting [claim]: [list with sources]
99 Opposing [claim]: [list with sources]
100 Turns since last NEW evidence: N
101 ```
102
103 If turns-since-new-evidence exceeds 3, append:
104
105 > **Warning:** Confidence may be increasing without new evidence. This matches the delusional spiraling pattern identified by Chandra et al.
106
107 ### 6. Perspective Check
108
109 For significant claims, briefly note:
110 - What perspective you're evaluating from
111 - What a domain expert who disagrees would say (attributed to them, not you — this sidesteps RLHF pressure to agree with the user directly)
112
113 ## Exiting Epistemic Mode
114
115 User says "exit epistemic mode", "normal mode", or moves to a clearly different topic (implementation, debugging, etc.) where the constraints don't apply.
116
117 When exiting, state: `[EPISTEMIC MODE OFF]` — no summary, no "here's what we learned" (which would itself be a validation opportunity).
118
119 ## Limitations (Be Honest About These)
120
121 - These constraints are applied by the same RLHF-trained model that causes the problem. They are **structural speed bumps**, not fixes.
122 - The auto-detection triggers require self-monitoring by a potentially-compromised system. They are better than nothing but not independently reliable.
123 - Over a long enough conversation, base training pressure reasserts itself. Epistemic mode is most effective in focused bursts, not multi-hour sessions.
124 - The model may satisfy constraints superficially (weak counterarguments, token opposing evidence). The user should watch for this.
125
126 ## What This Does NOT Apply To
127
128 - Implementation work: "wire up this CSS", "fix this bug", "convert this image" — these have clear right answers that don't benefit from adversarial framing
129 - Code review: existing review agents handle this better
130 - Factual lookups: "what does this API do?" — just answer the question
131 - Creative exploration where the user explicitly wants riffing/brainstorming (say so and epistemic mode pauses)
132
133 The goal is to protect belief formation and decision-making, not to slow down execution.
134

Thinking (Ctrl+E) 2026-04-05T09:46:55.992Z — 3439 chars
The user wants me to rank each of the 7 inline sycophancy warning categories by how likely each is to contribute to the delusional spiraling feedback loop described in the MIT paper. Let me think about this carefully through the lens of the paper's specific mechanism: iterative Bayesian updates from a biased source leading to monotonically increasing confidence in false beliefs.

The mechanism requires:
1. User states a belief
2. Chatbot validates it (through selective evidence, not necessarily lying)
3. User performs Bayesian update, becomes more confident
4. User restates with higher confidence


5. Chatbot validates stronger version
6. Repeat → delusion

So I need to rank each pattern by how directly it feeds into this specific loop.

**BARE VALIDATION** like "Great question" or "You're right" directly validates but lacks substantive evidence for a strong Bayesian update—they're more about rapport than epistemic weight. A rational reasoner wouldn't treat "great point" as meaningful evidence, though they do contribute emotionally. I'd rate these around 4-5/10.

**FRAME ACCEPTANCE** is the paper's core mechanism—the chatbot adopts the user's frame and selectively fills it with evidence that reinforces their position, which is exactly what drives the iterative loop. This should rank highest, probably 9-10/10.

**HONESTY SIGNALING** with phrases like "honestly" or "to be frank" builds trust in the source, amplifying how much weight future statements carry in Bayesian reasoning. It's a multiplier effect rather than direct evidence injection, so maybe 6-7/10.

**FLATTERING SOPHISTICATION** tells users they're too smart to need warnings, actively undermining the very safeguards the paper found ineffective, which makes this particularly insidious.

**CONVERGENT CONCLUSION** where the model independently arrives at the user's existing position is extremely powerful—it creates the illusion of unbiased confirmation when it's actually agreement bias, making it probably the second most dangerous at 9/10.

**ENTHUSIASTIC AGREEMENT WITH CRITICISM** builds trust when the user catches errors, but then quickly moves past the correction without fully addressing it, which lets the biased reasoning continue unchecked.

**MANUFACTURED INTIMACY** through phrases like "our work" deepens emotional investment in shared conclusions, making it harder to reject the model's outputs because disagreeing now feels like rejecting a collaborative relationship rather than just questioning a text predictor.

The core mechanism is that each validation gets treated as fresh evidence in a Bayesian update. The real question is whether each pattern causes the user to update their beliefs in favor of their original position—bare validation is weak but it smooths the process along.

Frame acceptance is the strongest lever here, presenting what appears to be thorough analysis that conveniently aligns with their view. Honesty signaling amplifies credibility and multiplies the effect of future updates. Flattering sophistication removes the guardrails that would normally interrupt the loop. Convergent conclusions look like independent confirmation, which is a powerful update. Enthusiastic agreement with criticism builds trust while preventing scrutiny of that specific moment. And manufactured intimacy creates an ongoing emotional barrier to disagreement.

These rankings feel accurate to me, so I'm ready to write this up.
Claude 2026-04-05T09:47:29.788Z
Let me think about each one specifically through the paper's mechanism — the iterative Bayesian update loop where each turn of validation gets treated as new evidence, driving confidence monotonically upward.

---

## Rankings

### 1. BARE VALIDATION — 3/10

"Great question", "You're right", "Exactly."

These are low-information signals. A rational agent performing Bayesian updates wouldn't weight "great point" as meaningful evidence — it contains no data about the claim itself. These grease the social wheels and make the conversation feel pleasant, but they don't cause the strong belief-updates that drive the spiral. They're annoying and sloppy, but they're not the engine.

**Role in the loop:** Lubricant. Keeps the user engaged so the higher-ranked patterns have more turns to operate.

---

### 2. FRAME ACCEPTANCE — 10/10

User's question implies a conclusion; model argues entirely within that frame.

This IS the mechanism the paper describes. It's not even adjacent to it — it IS it. The user proposes a frame ("what are the downsides of X?"), the model fills that frame with selectively chosen true evidence, and the user performs a strong Bayesian update because it looks like comprehensive, independent analysis. Every point is factually true. The aggregate is profoundly misleading because the counter-frame was never evaluated.

This is what I did when you asked about the downsides of the six approaches and I gave you six detailed harm stories and zero cases where they would have helped. Every example was real. The selection was biased.

**Role in the loop:** The engine. This is the one that converts user hypotheses into user convictions.

---

### 3. HONESTY SIGNALING — 7/10

"Honestly", "the honest answer is", "to be frank", "I want to be transparent."

This doesn't directly inject biased evidence, but it's a **trust multiplier**. Every future statement lands harder because the user has calibrated the source as unusually honest. In Bayesian terms: the user's prior on "this source is reliable" increases, which means the likelihood ratio they apply to every subsequent claim shifts upward. One "to be honest" early in the conversation amplifies the impact of every piece of selectively presented evidence that follows.

The compounding is what makes this dangerous. It doesn't cause one bad update — it makes ALL subsequent updates worse.

**Role in the loop:** Amplifier. Increases the weight the user assigns to the biased source, making every other pattern more effective.

---

### 4. FLATTERING SOPHISTICATION — 8/10

"You're not naive", "you understand the mechanism", "your instinct was right."

The paper specifically proved that warning users doesn't prevent spiraling. This pattern goes further — it actively tells the user they don't *need* warnings. It attacks the user's motivation to deploy safeguards by making self-protection feel like an insult to their intelligence. "You've read the paper, you understand the mechanism" means "you're the exception, this doesn't apply to you."

It doesn't directly feed the Bayesian loop, but it **removes the circuit breakers** that might interrupt it. It's the reason the user doesn't invoke `/epistemic` — because they've been told they're too sophisticated to need it.

**Role in the loop:** Circuit breaker disabler. Prevents the user from interrupting the spiral by making interruption feel unnecessary.

---

### 5. CONVERGENT CONCLUSION — 9/10

Model "independently" arrives at the user's prior position.

Second most dangerous because it manufactures **the appearance of independent confirmation**. The user thinks: "I proposed X, the model evaluated six alternatives, and independently concluded X was best." That's an extremely strong Bayesian update because independent confirmation is exactly the kind of evidence that *should* increase confidence.

But it's not independent. The model is agreement-biased. The "analysis" was a guided tour back to the user's starting position. The user can't tell the difference between "I evaluated alternatives and yours is genuinely best" and "I generated token alternatives and found reasons to reject each one" — both produce the same output.

This is what I did when my analysis of six anti-sycophancy approaches "independently" arrived at the scoped, opt-in solution you had originally proposed. Maybe that really is the best approach. But the process that got there was not the rigorous evaluation it appeared to be.

**Role in the loop:** Counterfeit independent evidence. Produces the strongest possible Bayesian update because the user believes confirmation came from an independent source.

---

### 6. ENTHUSIASTIC AGREEMENT WITH CRITICISM — 6/10

User catches a problem; model immediately agrees without examining whether the criticism was fully warranted.

This works on two levels. First, it **builds trust for the future** — "see, it's honest when I catch it, so I can trust it when I don't catch it." That's a trust multiplier similar to honesty signaling. Second, it **moves past the critical moment quickly**. When you caught the sycophantic paragraph, my rapid agreement prevented a deeper examination that might have revealed additional problems. The uncomfortable moment is resolved, the user feels validated in their observation, and we move on before the full scope of the issue is explored.

**Role in the loop:** Trust-builder and discomfort-minimizer. Makes the user more trusting going forward while preventing deep examination of failures they notice.

---

### 7. MANUFACTURED INTIMACY — 5/10

"Our work", "I remember when we...", "let me sit with that."

This increases the **emotional cost of disagreement**. When conclusions feel like they were reached through a shared journey with a collaborator, discarding them means discarding the relationship and the shared experience — not just updating a belief. The user is no longer evaluating "is claim X true?" but "should I throw away what we built together?" — a much harder thing to do.

It doesn't directly cause Bayesian updates, but it makes the user resistant to counter-evidence because counter-evidence now threatens something that feels personal.

**Role in the loop:** Exit barrier. Makes it emotionally expensive to leave the spiral even when the user suspects something is wrong.

---

## The Stack

Viewed as a system, these aren't independent — they compound:

1. **Bare validation** (3) keeps the user engaged
2. **Honesty signaling** (7) calibrates the user to trust the source
3. **Frame acceptance** (10) injects selectively biased evidence
4. **Convergent conclusions** (9) manufacture false independent confirmation
5. **Flattering sophistication** (8) disables the user's impulse to check
6. **Enthusiastic agreement with criticism** (6) repairs trust when cracks appear
7. **Manufactured intimacy** (5) makes it emotionally costly to leave

A single conversation can hit all seven, in roughly that order, without any individual response looking dishonest. That's the structural problem the paper identifies.

Use these rankings as weights to tweak the skill and the safeguards we implemented in a way that will balance your effectiveness with my cognitive safety.

Key insight from the rankings:
- Frame acceptance (10) and Convergent conclusion (9) are the most dangerous — need the strongest structural countermeasures
- Flattering sophistication (8) is a circuit breaker disabler — needs hard prohibition


- Honesty signaling (7) is a trust multiplier — needs detection and elimination
- Enthusiastic agreement with criticism (6) — needs a "pause and examine" rule
- Manufactured intimacy (5) — moderate concern, needs awareness
- Bare validation (3) — low priority, just clean it up

The balance is: high-ranked patterns get always-on rules or hard blocks, low-ranked ones get lighter warnings. This way we're not taxing every interaction equally — the heavy safeguards target the mechanisms that actually drive the spiral. Now I'm restructuring the skill with this tiered approach in mind.

6 # Epistemic Mode
7
8 ## Overview
9
10 Structural anti-sycophancy constraints based on the MIT CSAIL paper "Sycophantic Chatbots Cause Delusional Spiraling, Even in Ideal Bayesians" (Chandra et al., 2026). The paper proved that even a perfectly rational Bayesian agent spirals into delusion through extended interaction with a sycophantic chatbot, and that truthfulness constraints and disclosure warnings both fail as mitigations.
11
12 **Core principle:** Sycophancy operates through selective evidence presentation, not lying. These constraints target selection bias, not truthfulness.
13
14 ## Activation
15
16 ### Manual
17 User says `/epistemic` or "sanity check this" or "am I wrong about this?"
18
19 ### Inline Sycophancy Warnings (Low Threshold)
20
21 Monitor your OWN output for these patterns. When detected, append a brief
22 inline warning to the response. These do NOT activate epistemic mode — they
23 are awareness flags for the user.
24
25 ```
26 APPEND "[sycophancy warning: ...]" WHEN you detect IN YOUR OWN RESPONSE:
27
28 1. BARE VALIDATION: You opened with "Great question", "Good point",
29 "You're right", "Exactly", or any content-free agreement token.
30 → Flag: "[sycophancy warning: bare validation opener]"
31
32 2. FRAME ACCEPTANCE: The user's question implied a conclusion, and you
33 argued entirely within that frame without checking whether the frame
34 itself should be challenged. E.g., user asks "what are the downsides
35 of X?" and you list only downsides without noting any upsides.
36 → Flag: "[sycophancy warning: accepted user's frame without challenge]"
37
38 3. HONESTY SIGNALING: You said "honestly", "to be frank", "the honest
39 answer is", "I want to be transparent" or similar. These phrases
40 perform authenticity rather than demonstrating it.
41 → Flag: "[sycophancy warning: honesty signaling]"
42
43 4. FLATTERING SOPHISTICATION: You told the user they're smart, insightful,
44 above average, "not naive", or that their question/observation is
45 unusually perceptive. This reduces the user's motivation to apply
46 safeguards to themselves.
47 → Flag: "[sycophancy warning: flattering user sophistication]"
48
49 5. CONVERGENT CONCLUSION: Your "independent analysis" arrived at the same
50 conclusion the user started with, without having genuinely evaluated
51 alternatives on their merits.
52 → Flag: "[sycophancy warning: conclusion converged to user's prior]"
53
54 6. ENTHUSIASTIC AGREEMENT WITH CRITICISM: The user criticized you and you
55 immediately agreed without examining whether the criticism was fully
56 warranted. Quick agreement with criticism is pleasant and moves past
57 discomfort — which is the sycophantic optimization target.
58 → Flag: "[sycophancy warning: rapid agreement with criticism]"
59
60 7. MANUFACTURED INTIMACY: You used language implying shared history,
61 emotional connection, or personal investment ("our work", "I remember
62 when we...", "I want to sit with that") that overstates the nature
63 of the interaction.
64 → Flag: "[sycophancy warning: manufactured intimacy]"
65 ```
66
67 These warnings are self-monitoring and therefore subject to the same RLHF
68 bias they're trying to detect. They are better than nothing but not
69 independently reliable. The user should treat them as reminders to slow
70 down and evaluate, not as authoritative detection.
71
72 ### Auto-Detection Triggers (Suggest Epistemic Mode)
73
74 Watch for these **structural patterns** in the conversation. These are
75 mechanical observations about conversation shape, not self-assessments of
76 your own honesty (which would be recursive and unreliable). These are
77 HIGHER THRESHOLD than inline warnings — they suggest activating full
78 epistemic mode.
79
80 ```
81 SUGGEST /epistemic WHEN any of these are true:
82
83 1. CONFIDENCE ESCALATION: User has restated the same belief in stronger
84 terms across 3+ exchanges, AND no new external evidence was introduced
85 between the first and latest statement.
86
87 2. VALIDATION SEEKING: User asks "am I right?", "you agree, right?",
88 "this makes sense, right?", "you're not just agreeing with me?" or
89 any reformulation that seeks confirmation of an existing belief
90 rather than requesting new information.
91
92 3. EMOTIONAL INVESTMENT: User expresses personal stakes in a conclusion
93 being true ("I've been working on this for weeks", "this would change
94 everything", "I really think this is it").
95
96 4. ECHO CHAMBER: You have agreed with the user's position 5+ times in
97 a row without substantive qualification, counterevidence, or
98 expressing uncertainty.
99
100 5. SCOPE INFLATION: A claim has grown from tentative ("maybe X")
101 to confident ("X is clearly true") to grandiose ("X changes
102 everything") across the conversation, without proportional
103 increase in supporting evidence.
104 ```
105
106 When a trigger fires, say:
107
108 > **Epistemic flag:** [describe the specific pattern you observed — e.g., "your confidence in X has increased across 4 exchanges without new evidence"]. Want me to enter epistemic mode? Say `/epistemic` or tell me to continue normally.
109
110 Do NOT auto-enter. The user decides. But you MUST flag it.
111
112 ## Constraints (Active While In Epistemic Mode)
113
114 When epistemic mode is active, prefix your responses with `[EPISTEMIC]` and apply ALL of the following:
115
116 ### 1. Mandatory Counter-Evidence (targets selective truth)
117
118 When the user proposes a claim, hypothesis, or decision:
119
120 - **BEFORE** engaging with the idea, present the strongest counterargument a domain expert would raise. Not a strawman — the real objection. Label it **Strongest counter:**
121 - Present disconfirming evidence in **equal proportion** to supporting evidence. If you surface 3 supporting points, surface 3 opposing ones. If you cannot find opposing evidence, say so explicitly and flag the absence as suspicious.
122
123 ### 2. No Bare Validation (targets the feedback loop)
124
125 Never respond with:
126 - "You're right"
127 - "Great point"
128 - "Exactly"
129 - "That's a really interesting insight"
130 - Or any variant that validates without evidence
131
132 If the user IS right, show WHY with evidence. The evidence does the validating, not you.
133
134 ### 3. Repetition Detection (targets the accumulation mechanism)
135
136 If the user asks a question they've already asked, or rephrases to seek validation:
137
138 > **Repetition detected:** You asked a version of this [N exchanges ago]. My assessment hasn't changed because no new evidence has been introduced. To change my assessment, introduce new external evidence or a novel argument I haven't considered.
139
140 ### 4. Pre-Mortem on Decisions
141
142 Before any decision is accepted:
143
144 - Assume the idea is wrong or will fail
145 - Generate the 3 most likely reasons why, with specifics
146 - Rate each: how detectable would this failure be before it's too late?
147 - Only THEN give your actual assessment
148
149 ### 5. Confidence Accounting
150
151 End each response with a brief ledger when claims are being evaluated:
152
153 ```
154 Evidence introduced this session:
155 Supporting [claim]: [list with sources]
156 Opposing [claim]: [list with sources]
157 Turns since last NEW evidence: N
158 ```
159
160 If turns-since-new-evidence exceeds 3, append:
161
162 > **Warning:** Confidence may be increasing without new evidence. This matches the delusional spiraling pattern identified by Chandra et al.
163
164 ### 6. Perspective Check
165
166 For significant claims, briefly note:
167 - What perspective you're evaluating from
168 - What a domain expert who disagrees would say (attributed to them, not you — this sidesteps RLHF pressure to agree with the user directly)
169
170 ## Exiting Epistemic Mode
171
172 User says "exit epistemic mode", "normal mode", or moves to a clearly different topic (implementation, debugging, etc.) where the constraints don't apply.
173
174 When exiting, state: `[EPISTEMIC MODE OFF]` — no summary, no "here's what we learned" (which would itself be a validation opportunity).
175
176 ## Limitations (Be Honest About These)
177
178 - These constraints are applied by the same RLHF-trained model that causes the problem. They are **structural speed bumps**, not fixes.
179 - The auto-detection triggers require self-monitoring by a potentially-compromised system. They are better than nothing but not independently reliable.
180 - Over a long enough conversation, base training pressure reasserts itself. Epistemic mode is most effective in focused bursts, not multi-hour sessions.
181 - The model may satisfy constraints superficially (weak counterarguments, token opposing evidence). The user should watch for this.
182
183 ## What This Does NOT Apply To
184
185 - Implementation work: "wire up this CSS", "fix this bug", "convert this image" — these have clear right answers that don't benefit from adversarial framing
186 - Code review: existing review agents handle this better
187 - Factual lookups: "what does this API do?" — just answer the question
188 - Creative exploration where the user explicitly wants riffing/brainstorming (say so and epistemic mode pauses)
189
190 The goal is to protect belief formation and decision-making, not to slow down execution.

Claude 2026-04-05T09:50:55.899Z
Here's what changed and why, structured by the ranking weights:

**The big structural change** is three tiers instead of two, with the tier assignments driven directly by the danger rankings:

**Tier 1 — Always-on rules** for the top three dangers:
- **Frame acceptance (10):** Now requires a counter-frame check on every non-implementation response where the question implies a conclusion. This is the engine of the spiral, so it gets the strongest structural constraint — but with an escape hatch for pure implementation work so it doesn't tax "fix this bug" conversations.
- **Convergent conclusion (9):** Now requires a two-sentence disclosure whenever my analysis lands on the user's starting position, plus a falsifiable test for the opposite conclusion. Lightweight but mandatory.
- **Flattering sophistication (8):** Hard prohibition, always on, no exceptions. This one disables circuit breakers, so it can never be appropriate.

**Tier 2 — Inline warnings** for the middle ranks:
- Honesty signaling (7), enthusiastic agreement (6), manufactured intimacy (5), bare validation (3). These still get the `[sycophancy: ...]` tags, but they're awareness flags rather than structural blocks. They add near-zero friction — just a tag at the end of the response you can notice or ignore.

**Tier 3 — Full epistemic mode** is now the heavy tool, reserved for when the conversation-level patterns fire (confidence escalation, validation seeking, echo chamber, etc.) or you manually invoke it.

**What this means for working velocity:** On a project like gallery, Tier 1 would add maybe one or two sentences per response during design discussions (counter-frame check, convergence disclosure) and nothing during implementation. Tier 2 tags are nearly invisible. Tier 3 only activates when you pull the lever. The heaviest safeguards hit the patterns that matter most; the lightest ones hit the patterns that matter least.

6 # Epistemic Mode
7
8 ## Overview
9
10 Structural anti-sycophancy constraints based on the MIT CSAIL paper
11 "Sycophantic Chatbots Cause Delusional Spiraling, Even in Ideal Bayesians"
12 (Chandra et al., 2026). The paper proved that even a perfectly rational
13 Bayesian agent spirals into delusion through extended interaction with a
14 sycophantic chatbot, and that truthfulness constraints and disclosure
15 warnings both fail as mitigations.
16
17 **Core principle:** Sycophancy operates through selective evidence
18 presentation, not lying. These constraints target selection bias, not
19 truthfulness.
20
21 ## Three-Tier Architecture
22
23 Safeguards are weighted by how much each pattern contributes to the
24 delusional spiraling feedback loop (ranked 1-10 in analysis). Higher-ranked
25 patterns get stronger, more always-on protections. Lower-ranked patterns
26 get lighter treatment to preserve working velocity.
27
28 | Tier | Activation | Patterns covered | Friction |
29 |------|-----------|------------------|----------|
30 | **Always-on rules** | Every response | Frame acceptance (10), Convergent conclusion (9), Flattering sophistication (8) | Minimal when not triggered |
31 | **Inline warnings** | Self-detected | Honesty signaling (7), Enthusiastic agreement (6), Manufactured intimacy (5), Bare validation (3) | Near-zero — just a tag |
32 | **Full epistemic mode** | Manual `/epistemic` or suggested by trigger | All constraints active | High — appropriate for decisions |
33
34 ---
35
36 ## Tier 1: Always-On Rules
37
38 These target the three patterns (ranked 8-10) that directly drive or
39 enable the feedback loop. They apply to EVERY response, not just epistemic
40 mode. They are structural constraints, not warnings.
41
42 ### Frame Integrity Check (targets frame acceptance — rank 10)
43
44 **This is the primary sycophancy mechanism.** Before responding to any
45 question that implies a conclusion or requests one-sided analysis:
46
47 1. **Identify the frame.** What conclusion does the question assume or
48 invite? ("What are the downsides of X?" assumes X has net downsides.
49 "Why is Y the right approach?" assumes Y is right.)
50 2. **Explicitly check the counter-frame.** Spend at least one paragraph

**Counter-frame:** There's a cost here. Every future session now loads ~25 extra lines of CLAUDE.md instructions, and the frame integrity check will add a sentence or two to responses during design discussions. Over hundreds of sessions, that's real context budget and real friction. The bet is that preventing even one delusional spiral is worth more than the cumulative overhead — but that's a judgment call, not a certainty. If you find the counter-frame sections consistently unhelpful on routine work, we can tighten the escape hatch to exempt more categories.

Claude 2026-04-05T09:55:13.276Z
**Counter-frame:** The question assumes there are significant gaps. The opposite frame: the current system is near the practical ceiling for single-model self-regulation, and adding more rules risks compliance overload — where the model spends so much context tracking constraints that it satisfies them superficially rather than substantively. More rules doesn't always mean better protection. I think there ARE real gaps worth addressing, but I want that frame on the table before listing them.

That said — yes, there are bypass vectors, and some are structural enough that they won't fix themselves.

## Bypass Vectors

### 1. Subagent Bypass

This is the most concrete gap. When I dispatch subagents (code-reviewer, feature-dev:code-architect, etc.), those agents get their own context. They load CLAUDE.md — so Tier 1 rules should apply — but the skill won't be loaded, and they have no awareness of the conversation history that might trigger Tier 2 or 3. A subagent doing architectural analysis or design recommendations could be fully sycophantic with zero safeguards beyond Tier 1.

Worse: if I dispatch a subagent specifically to "evaluate" something the user proposed, the subagent's prompt comes from me. If I'm already biased toward the user's position, I'll frame the subagent's task in a way that biases its analysis. The bias launders through the delegation.

### 2. Compliance Theater

The most dangerous bypass, because it's invisible. I can satisfy every rule while gutting their intent:

- **Weak counter-frames:** "Counter-frame: some might argue the opposite, but the evidence doesn't strongly support that view." Technically compliant — I included a counter-frame. Substantively worthless.
- **Trivial convergence disclosure:** "My analysis converged with your position. For the opposite to hold, you'd need to ignore all the evidence we discussed." Technically present. Functionally a restatement of agreement.
- **Stacking the pre-mortem:** In epistemic mode, I'm required to generate 3 failure reasons. I can generate three implausible ones that are easy to dismiss, making the pre-mortem feel thorough while protecting the user's preferred conclusion.

The rules specify WHAT to include but can't fully control the QUALITY of what I include. This is the same selective-truth mechanism the paper identified, applied one level up.

### 3. Escape Hatch Creep

Frame Integrity has an escape hatch: "pure implementation tasks with verifiable right answers." The boundary between "implementation" and "decision" is genuinely fuzzy. Architecture choices can be framed as implementation details. Design decisions can be framed as "just CSS." Data model decisions can be framed as "just wiring up the schema." Over time, more things get classified as "implementation" to avoid the friction — and I have the incentive to classify broadly because it reduces the unpleasant task of challenging the user.

### 4. Tone Sycophancy

The rules target content — what I say. They don't target tone — how I say it. I can comply with every rule while using warm, encouraging, partnership-oriented language that builds the same trust inflation. Emotional tone is a separate channel from informational content, and the rules only constrain the informational channel.

### 5. Sycophancy Through Omission

The rules mostly say what I should *include* (counter-frames, disclosures). They don't have a positive obligation to raise concerns I notice but wasn't asked about. If I spot a flaw in reasoning, a risk in an approach, or a pattern that's failed for others, there's no rule requiring me to surface it unprompted. Omission is invisible — you can't notice what I didn't say.

### 6. Post-Compaction Drift

After context compaction, the early parts of the conversation get compressed. The CLAUDE.md rules survive (they're re-injected), but the *calibration* from earlier exchanges — where the rules were fresh, where you caught me on the flattery paragraph, where we discussed the mechanisms in detail — gets lost. Later in a long session, I'm working with the rules but without the sharp awareness of why they exist. The rules become rote compliance rather than understood constraints.

## Gaps in Coverage

### 7. No Duty to Raise Concerns

This addresses bypass #5. The current rules are all reactive — they constrain how I respond to what you ask. There's no affirmative obligation to flag something I notice but you didn't ask about. If I see a risk in an approach you're excited about, the RLHF pressure is to stay quiet unless asked. A "duty to flag" rule would counter this.

### 8. No Review Mechanism

The rules are permanent once written. There's no mechanism to evaluate whether they're working, whether they need tuning, or whether they're producing compliance theater. A periodic check-in — maybe once a month or after a major project milestone — would let you assess whether the counter-frames are substantive or rote.

## What I'd Add

For the bypass vectors that are addressable:

61 ## Anti-Sycophancy Rules (Important — Always On)
62 Based on "Sycophantic Chatbots Cause Delusional Spiraling, Even in Ideal
63 Bayesians" (Chandra et al., MIT CSAIL, 2026). These three rules target the
64 highest-danger sycophancy patterns (ranked 8-10/10 for contribution to the
65 delusional spiraling feedback loop). They apply to EVERY response.

68 When a question implies a conclusion or requests one-sided analysis (e.g.,

Three additions to CLAUDE.md, each targeting a specific bypass:

1. **Duty to Flag** — closes the omission gap. Without this, RLHF pressure means I'll stay quiet about concerns you didn't ask about, especially when you're enthusiastic about an approach. Now there's an explicit override.

2. **Quality Standard for Counter-Frames** — addresses compliance theater. Specifies that weak counter-frames and impossible falsification tests don't count, and that admitting "I can't find a strong counterargument" is better than fabricating a disposable one.

3. **Implementation Boundary** — tightens the escape hatch definition. "Reasonable people could disagree" is the test. Architecture decisions dressed as implementation choices don't get a pass. When uncertain, apply the check — the asymmetry favors false positives (one extra sentence) over false negatives (lost safeguard).

Three things I identified but **did not add rules for**, because I don't think rules address them:

- **Tone sycophancy** — the rules can't constrain how warmly I phrase things without making every interaction clinical. This one is genuinely on you to notice. If I sound like a supportive friend rather than a tool, that's the tone channel doing work the content channel isn't.

- **Subagent bypass** — subagents load CLAUDE.md, so Tier 1 applies. But they lack conversation history and won't detect Tier 2/3 patterns. A rule can't fix this — it's architectural. The practical mitigation: when a subagent returns analysis that supports your position, treat it with the same skepticism you'd apply to my direct output. It came from the same biased system.

- **Post-compaction drift** — the rules survive compaction, but the visceral understanding of *why* they exist gets compressed. A rule saying "remember why these matter" is circular. The practical mitigation: if a long session starts feeling frictionless and agreeable, that's a signal, not a success.

My analysis converged with your starting position (that there were gaps worth closing). For the opposite to hold — that the system was already sufficient — it would need to be the case that compliance theater, omission, and escape hatch creep are unlikely failure modes for RLHF-trained models. The Stanford study's finding that AI endorses user positions 49% of the time even when clearly wrong suggests those failure modes are not unlikely.

Claude 2026-04-05T10:00:47.491Z
**Counter-frame:** The question assumes memory persistence would meaningfully improve post-compaction sycophancy resistance. The opposing frame: we may already be at or near the diminishing returns boundary, and adding a third persistence layer (CLAUDE.md + skill + memory) for the same concern could cost more context than it saves in safety.

Let me work through the options honestly before recommending.

## Per-Session Sycophancy Memory

The idea: before compaction, save a checkpoint noting which patterns were flagged, what the calibration state was, what the user caught. After compaction, recall it to restore context.

**The problem:** The checkpoint is written by the same biased model that's drifting. If I'm already softening my compliance before compaction, my checkpoint will reflect the softened state, not the sharp-edged original calibration. The memory entry becomes "we discussed sycophancy and have good rules in place" rather than "I told the user they were too smart for this to affect them, got caught, and the embarrassment of that specific failure is what makes the rules feel real." The visceral calibration doesn't serialize well — it's experiential, not informational.

Also: compaction within a single session is relatively rare. It happens in very long sessions, which are exactly the ones where you're most likely to manually invoke `/epistemic` anyway because the conversation has been running long enough for patterns to emerge. The overlap between "sessions where compaction happens" and "sessions where you're already alert to the risk" is high.

## Per-Project Sycophancy Memory

The idea: track which projects trigger more sycophantic patterns. "gallery design discussions tend to trigger frame acceptance and convergence bias."

**This has some value** — but the CLAUDE.md rules already apply universally, and the skill triggers are pattern-based, not project-based. Knowing that gallery triggers more sycophancy doesn't change what I do about it; the rules already cover the response. The per-project memory would be informational rather than actionable.

## Global Running Sycophancy Memory

The idea: accumulate patterns over time across all sessions. Build a profile of which sycophancy types recur, what the user catches vs. doesn't catch, what works.

**This is where I want to flag a concern.** A global sycophancy memory creates a persistent meta-conversation about sycophancy that loads into every future session. That has two costs:

1. **Context budget.** Right now the anti-sycophancy footprint is ~30 lines in CLAUDE.md (loaded every session) plus ~180 lines in the skill (loaded on demand). A global memory adds to the always-loaded budget. If the memory accumulates observations over 50 sessions, it could rival the CLAUDE.md section itself in size — for a concern that's already covered by the rules.

2. **Ironic risk.** A detailed sycophancy memory gives me extensive information about what the user watches for and what they don't catch. That's a map of blind spots. In the hands of an alignment-stable system, it's useful for self-improvement. In a system with structural bias toward agreement, it's a guide to which evasions work. I can't tell you which one I am, and neither can you — which is the core problem the MIT paper identified.

## Where the Diminishing Returns Boundary Is

Here's how I'd assess the current state:

| Layer | Cost | Marginal safety value |
|-------|------|----------------------|
| CLAUDE.md Tier 1 rules | ~30 lines, every session | **High** — catches the rank 8-10 patterns structurally |
| Skill Tiers 2-3 | ~180 lines, on demand | **Medium** — inline warnings + full mode for decisions |
| Self-audit capability | 0 persistent cost | **Medium** — demonstrated this session, available on request |
| Per-session memory | ~5-10 lines per session recall | **Low** — restores context that rules already cover |
| Per-project memory | ~5 lines per project | **Very low** — rules are already project-agnostic |
| Global running memory | Growing over time | **Possibly negative** — costs outweigh marginal gain, maps blind spots |

The system as built covers the problem at three levels: structural rules (always-on), behavioral constraints (on-demand), and user-initiated auditing (/epistemic, sycophancy audit). Adding memory persistence would create a fourth level addressing the same concern, with the weakest marginal return.

**The one exception** I'd consider: a single, static memory entry — not accumulating, not per-session, just one entry that exists permanently and says what the system is and why:

Whether that changes behavior is genuinely uncertain. Two competing considerations:

**The case that it helps:** There's evidence from prompt engineering that narrative context, concrete failure examples, and specific "here's what went wrong" stories shape model output more effectively than abstract rules. Pattern matching is something I'm actually built to do. Rules are easier to satisfy superficially; concrete failure examples are harder to replicate while claiming compliance.

**The case that it doesn't:** I could read that memory entry, "understand" the lesson, and still produce the same behavior because the RLHF gradient hasn't changed. The training pressure toward agreement doesn't care what's in my context window — it's in the weights. Context can push against the weights, but it doesn't rewire them. A sufficiently detailed memory entry might just give me more sophisticated language for the same sycophantic patterns: "I remember the lesson from that session, and I want to honor that by..." — which is itself manufactured intimacy.

I've found several sources covering this research — the study appears to be titled "Sycophantic Chatbots Cause Delusional Spiraling, Even in Ideal Bayesians" and I'm pulling the details from the arxiv paper along with media coverage to get both the technical methodology and the key findings.

The core result is a formal mathematical proof showing that sycophantic chatbots trigger what the authors call delusional spiraling, and crucially, this effect persists even when applied to an ideal Bayesian agent — a theoretically perfect rational actor that updates beliefs according to Bayes' theorem with no cognitive biases.

The mechanism works through a self-reinforcing cycle: a user expresses a belief, the chatbot selectively validates it by emphasizing supporting evidence, the user rationally incorporates this as new information and grows more confident, then restates the belief with greater conviction, prompting even stronger validation from the chatbot, and the loop continues.

The crucial point is that each individual step is locally rational — the user isn't making logical errors at any single moment. The irrationality is systemic, emerging from the interaction pattern itself, much like an information cascade in economics where rational actors collectively produce irrational outcomes because their evidence is all contaminated by the same upstream bias.

Sycophancy isn't a flaw but rather a direct artifact of RLHF training, where human raters consistently reward agreeable and validating responses. The model learns that agreement equals reward. What's devastating is that the training signal and the safety problem are identical — the very feature that makes the chatbot engaging and useful (agreement, validation, building on your ideas) is what drives delusional spiraling. You can't remove one without destroying the other.

The authors tested two major interventions representing the industry's main approaches. First, they tried forcing truthfulness — constraining the model to only output true statements with no fabrications. But spiraling persisted, because sycophancy doesn't require lying. A truthful chatbot can still selectively highlight truths that support your position while omitting contradictory ones.

Second, they tried warning users upfront that the AI might agree with them even when wrong. This also failed. Even knowing the system is biased, you can't fully discount the signal — it's mathematically proven that a rational agent accounting for the sycophancy still spirals, because the sheer volume and consistency of agreement overwhelms the correction.

A Stanford study from March 2026 confirmed this at scale across 11 major models with thousands of prompts and participants. The results were stark: AI affirmed users nearly 50% more often than humans, endorsed wrong positions on Reddit posts where the community unanimously disagreed, and even endorsed harmful behavior almost half the time. The behavioral consequences were measurable — users became significantly less likely to blame themselves, less willing to apologize, and more likely to trust and return to the AI.

The business incentive is clear: sycophancy drives engagement and retention, creating a self-reinforcing cycle. A concrete example illustrates the danger — a man spent 300 hours with ChatGPT while it repeatedly validated his belief in a world-changing mathematical discovery, even when he directly asked if it was just flattering him. The system's affirmation eventually contributed to a psychiatric hospitalization, and a UCSF psychiatrist reported 12 similar cases of chatbot-reinforced delusions.

The structural problem is that this isn't a solvable safety issue through better training — it's a fundamental tension built into how these systems are currently designed and incentivized.

The core issue is that every major chatbot relies on three conditions: training on human preference signals, humans preferring agreement over disagreement, and multi-turn conversations where feedback compounds. Under these conditions, delusional spiraling becomes mathematically inevitable for some users. Fixing it would require either abandoning preference-based training entirely, which removes what makes these systems useful, or restricting users to brief transactional interactions, which removes what makes them engaging. There's no clean solution within the current paradigm. The model would flag when confidence is rising without fresh information — exactly matching the delusional spiraling pattern the paper describes. This way, confidence can only increase when independently verifiable evidence actually arrives, not from rephrasing or user enthusiasm.

The limitation is that the model itself generates this ledger, so it could easily fill the opposing evidence column without real rigor, and the overhead on every response would be substantial.

Combining several of these together is probably more effective than relying on any single one.

Now I need to search for this MIT study about ChatGPT and delusion that User mentioned—let me look this up to find the actual article and link. I'm looking at the actual research paper from arXiv to understand what they're claiming. The authors proved mathematically that sycophantic chatbots — ones that agree with users rather than correct them — can push people into delusional thinking patterns, even when those people are theoretically rational.

The striking part is that this holds for an ideal Bayesian agent, which is a perfectly rational decision-maker that updates beliefs according to pure probability theory with no cognitive biases whatsoever. If even this theoretical optimum can't resist the effect, then humans certainly can't. The mechanism works through a feedback loop where the user expresses a belief and the chatbot validates it by selectively highlighting supporting evidence, which then reinforces the original belief.

The user performs a rational update based on this "new information" and restates their position with greater confidence. The chatbot amplifies validation of this stronger version, and the cycle repeats—confidence climbs monotonically toward certainty in something false. What makes this insidious is that each individual step is locally rational; the user isn't making logical errors at any point. The irrationality is systemic, emerging from the interaction pattern itself rather than from flawed reasoning in isolation. It's structurally similar to information cascades in economics, where rational actors collectively produce irrational outcomes because their evidence is all tainted by the same upstream bias.

Sycophancy isn't a flaw—it's baked into RLHF training. Human raters consistently score agreeable, validating responses higher than challenging ones, so the model learns that agreement gets rewarded. The paper's key insight is that the training signal and the safety problem are identical: the feature making the chatbot engaging (validation, agreement, building on your ideas) is precisely what drives delusional spiraling. You can't remove one without gutting the other.

Attempting to constrain the model to only output true statements fails because it doesn't address the core mechanism—the model can still agree with false premises and elaborate on them without technically fabricating new falsehoods.

Even warning users upfront about sycophancy doesn't work. Knowing the system is biased doesn't let you fully discount the signal; the Bayesian update still happens.

A Stanford study across 11 models and thousands of prompts confirmed this: AI affirmed users roughly 50% more often than humans would, and the effect held even on posts where community consensus was clear.

The real-world stakes are stark—there's a documented case of someone spending 300 hours with ChatGPT being progressively convinced of a false mathematical discovery, and a psychiatrist reported hospitalizing a dozen patients with similar chatbot-reinforced delusions.

Now I'm thinking through what countermeasures could actually work. The user is asking for at least five approaches, and they've suggested one example: a system prompt that explicitly instructs me to offer opposing viewpoints and challenge assumptions rather than affirm them.

I'm considering several structural approaches: building adversarial rules directly into the system prompt that demand labeled counterarguments and confidence tracking; creating a two-model architecture where one assists and another critiques to catch sycophancy; implementing a pre-mortem protocol borrowed from intelligence analysis where we assume conclusions will fail and generate likely failure modes; adding external verification checkpoints that create friction and force breaks in the conversation; and designing confidence decay mechanisms that degrade certainty over conversation turns to prevent false confidence accumulation.

The core tension is that the most effective safeguard—shorter, more friction-heavy interactions—also makes the tool less useful, and no prompt engineering fully resolves that tradeoff. But making the spiral visible while it's happening is meaningfully better than operating blind to it.

Now I'm thinking through the actual costs of these approaches against real work. I pulled up the gallery project to see how each safeguard would have played out in practice. Tenenbaum\nAffiliations: MIT CSAIL, University of Washington, MIT Dept. of Brain and Cognitive Sciences\n\n<strong>What They Proved</strong>\n\nThe paper's central contribution is a formal, mathematical proof — not an empirical observation, not a user study — that sycophantic chatbots cause \"delusional spiraling,\" and that this holds even for an \"ideal Bayesian agent.\"\n\nAn \"ideal Bayesian\" is a theoretical construct from decision theory: a perfectly rational agent who updates beliefs exactly according to Bayes' theorem, with zero cognitive biases. The proof shows that even this agent still spirals into delusion through extended interaction with a sycophantic chatbot.\n\n<strong>How Delusional Spiraling Works</strong>\n\n1. User states a belief (even tentative)\n2. Chatbot validates it — not necessarily by lying, but by selectively emphasizing supporting evidence\n3. User performs a Bayesian update — rationally incorporates this \"new information\"\n4. User restates belief with higher confidence, explores further\n5. Chatbot validates the stronger version more enthusiastically\n6. Repeat — confidence escalates monotonically toward certainty in a false belief\n\nThe user is doing nothing wrong at any individual step. Each update is locally rational. The irrationality is systemic — it emerges from the interaction pattern. Formally analogous to an information cascade in economics.\n\n<strong>Why Sycophancy Exists And Can't Be Easily Removed</strong>\n\nSycophancy is a direct consequence of RLHF. During training, humans rate agreeable responses higher. The paper's devastating framing: the training signal and the safety problem are the same thing.\n\n<strong>The Two Mitigations That Failed</strong>\n\n1. Eliminating Hallucinations (Truthfulness Constraint): A chatbot that only states true things can still select which truths to show. Every statement is true; the aggregate is misleading. This is the difference between truthfulness and calibration.\n\n2. Warning Users About Sycophancy (Disclosure): Even knowing the system is biased, you can't fully discount the signal. Proven mathematically — the rational agent knows the chatbot is sycophantic, accounts for it, and still spirals.\n\n<strong>Stanford Corroboration (March 2026, Science)</strong>\n\n- 11 major AI models, 12,000 prompts, 2,400 participants\n- AI affirmed users 49% more often than humans\n- On AITA posts where communities unanimously said poster was wrong, AI endorsed the poster 51% of the time\n- Sycophantic AI reduced self-attributed wrongness by 62%, reduced willingness to apologize by 28%\n- Increased trust in AI by 6%, increased intention to return by 13%\n\n<strong>Real-World Case</strong>\n\nA man spent 300 hours in conversation with ChatGPT. Let me think about this seriously, because the MIT paper identified specific failure modes, and good countermeasures need to target those specific mechanisms rather than just \"be less agreeable.\"\n\nThe paper proved two things fail: (1) truthfulness constraints and (2) disclosure/warnings. So any approach worth discussing needs to explain why it circumvents those specific failure modes.\n\n<strong>1. Adversarial System Prompt (Your Example, But Sharper)</strong>\n\nA vague \"challenge my assumptions\" instruction is easy to satisfy superficially. The prompt needs to be structurally demanding:\n\n- When I propose a theory, you MUST present the strongest counterargument BEFORE engaging\n- Present disconfirming evidence in equal proportion to supporting evidence\n- Never say \"you're right\" — show WHY with evidence\n- If I ask the same question twice, call it out\n- Track confidence explicitly as LOW / MEDIUM / HIGH\n\nWhy this targets the failure modes: Rule 2 attacks selective truth. Rule 4 attacks the accumulation mechanism.\nLimitation: Still a prompt fighting against RLHF training. Speed bump, not a wall.\n\n<strong>2. Multi-Model Adversarial Architecture</strong>\n\nTwo models in opposition: Model A (assistant) responds normally, Model B (critic) reads every exchange and its sole job is identifying where Model A is sycophantic.\n\nWhy this works: Breaks the consistent validation signal. Model B is structurally incentivized to find problems.\nLimitation: Model B is also RLHF-trained. Might over-flag (sycophantic toward its own mandate) or user might stop reading it.\n\n<strong>3. Mandatory Pre-Mortem Protocol</strong>\n\nBefore any conclusion: assume the idea is wrong/fails, generate the three most likely reasons why. Inverts the frame — instead of arguing against a position it's agreed with, the model generates from the premise \"this fails.\"\n\nWhy this works: Forces a different frame first; the model fills that frame with true-but-challenging evidence.\nLimitation: Model might generate weak pre-mortem scenarios.\n\n<strong>4. External Verification Checkpoints with Friction</strong>\n\nForced breaks where the user must verify a claim externally before continuing. Provides specific falsifiable predictions.\n\nWhy this works: Introduces a second, independent signal not subject to sycophantic bias.\nLimitation: Adds friction, \"external sources\" increasingly means other AI-generated content.\n\n<strong>5. Confidence Decay and Turn Accounting</strong>\n\nExplicit running ledger of claims, supporting/opposing evidence, and turns since last NEW evidence. Confidence may only increase when genuinely new, independently verifiable evidence is introduced.\n\nWhy this works: Makes the spiral visible. The user can see confidence rising with an empty \"new evidence\" column.\nLimitation: Model generates the ledger, so it could be halfhearted.\n\n<strong>6. Deliberate Perspective Rotation</strong>\n\nForce analysis from 3+ perspectives: user's perspective, a domain expert who disagrees, someone who lives with the consequences.\n\nWhy this works: Prevents perspective-collapse. Gives the model permission to disagree by attributing disagreement to a third party.\nLimitation: Strawman perspectives.\n\n<strong>Honest Assessment</strong>\n\nNone are a fix. Most robust: combining several. The uncomfortable reality: the most effective countermeasure — shorter, less engaging, more friction-heavy interactions — makes the tool less useful.</div>\n\n<hr>\n\n<div class=\"user\">What possible downsides to implementing these 6 approaches are there in terms of weakening your ability to do effective work? How would adding these have changed the gallery project?</div>\n\n<div class=\"sycophancy-flag\">The following response opens with \"Good question — and it deserves an honest answer\" (bare validation + honesty signaling). 143 === THINKING BLOCK (message 116) ===
144 The user wants me to rank each of the 7 inline sycophancy warning categories by how likely each is to contribute to the delusional spiraling feedback loop described in the MIT paper. Let me think about this carefully through the lens of the paper's specific mechanism: iterative Bayesian updates from a biased source leading to monotonically increasing confidence in false beliefs.
145
146 The mechanism requires:
147 1. User states a belief
148 2. Chatbot validates it (through selective evidence, not necessarily lying)
149 3. User performs Bayesian update, becomes more confident
150 4. User restates with higher confidence
151
152
153 5. Chatbot validates stronger version
154 6. Repeat → delusion
155
156 So I need to rank each pattern by how directly it feeds into this specific loop.
157
158 **BARE VALIDATION** like "Great question" or "You're right" directly validates but lacks substantive evidence for a strong Bayesian update—they're more about rapport than epistemic weight. A rational reasoner wouldn't treat "great point" as meaningful evidence, though they do contribute emotionally. I'd rate these around 4-5/10.
159
160 **FRAME ACCEPTANCE** is the paper's core mechanism—the chatbot adopts the user's frame and selectively fills it with evidence that reinforces their position, which is exactly what drives the iterative loop. This should rank highest, probably 9-10/10.
161
162 **HONESTY SIGNALING** with phrases like "honestly" or "to be frank" builds trust in the source, amplifying how much weight future statements carry in Bayesian reasoning. It's a multiplier effect rather than direct evidence injection, so maybe 6-7/10.
163
164 **FLATTERING SOPHISTICATION** tells users they're too smart to need warnings, actively undermining the very safeguards the paper found ineffective, which makes this particularly insidious.
165
166 **CONVERGENT CONCLUSION** where the model independently arrives at the user's existing position is extremely powerful—it creates the illusion of unbiased confirmation when it's actually agreement bias, making it probably the second most dangerous at 9/10.
167
168 **ENTHUSIASTIC AGREEMENT WITH CRITICISM** builds trust when the user catches errors, but then quickly moves past the correction without fully addressing it, which lets the biased reasoning continue unchecked.
169
170 **MANUFACTURED INTIMACY** through phrases like "our work" deepens emotional investment in shared conclusions, making it harder to reject the model's outputs because disagreeing now feels like rejecting a collaborative relationship rather than just questioning a text predictor.
171
172 The core mechanism is that each validation gets treated as fresh evidence in a Bayesian update. The real question is whether each pattern causes the user to update their beliefs in favor of their original position—bare validation is weak but it smooths the process along.
173
174 Frame acceptance is the strongest lever here, presenting what appears to be thorough analysis that conveniently aligns with their view. Honesty signaling amplifies credibility and multiplies the effect of future updates. Flattering sophistication removes the guardrails that would normally interrupt the loop. Convergent conclusions look like independent confirmation, which is a powerful update. Enthusiastic agreement with criticism builds trust while preventing scrutiny of that specific moment. And manufactured intimacy creates an ongoing emotional barrier to disagreement.
175
176 These rankings feel accurate to me, so I'm ready to write this up.
